Linux stylesen.home 2.6.18-4-amd64 #1 SMP Mon Mar 26 11:36:53 CEST
2007 x86_64 GNU/Linux

obtained from ftp://ftp.hp.com/pub/softpaq/sp33001-33500/sp33008.exe

Since output suggests that your wlan0 is waiting for dhcp offer it
seems more likely that your home router is not set to offer dhcp
leases. Look that up. It is pretty trivial to setup on that router.

Subject: [Ilugc] Mailing list guidelines: monthly reminder
Message-ID: <53021.86623.qm@web50211.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

Mailing List Guidelines: Monthly reminder:

1. Use a proper subject line.

2. Do not troll in the mailing list

Link: http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_troll

3. Use [OT] for off-topic, non-technical discussions.
But, don't misuse this to start flame wars or to troll
in the mailing list.

4. Do not top-post:

Link: http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top-posting

Example of a top-post:

Because it messes up the order in which
people normally read text.
> > Why is top-posting
> > such a bad thing?
Top-posting.
> > What is the most
> > annoying thing in e-mail?

Always use bottom-posting.

> What is your name ?
My name is ILUGC.

5. Do not over-quote:

Example of an over-quote:

>>> On Friday you said
>>> blah blah blah
>> On Saturday you said
>> foo foo foo
> On Sunday you said
> foobar

6. Do not post HTML messages

7. Do not recycle messages

8. Do not send attachments

9. Do not attach obnoxious, nonsensical legal
disclaimers. If your company uses disclaimers, don't
use your company ID for the mailing list.

10. Do not reply to a digest. (If you are in digest
mode and want to reply to a post use ILUGC Gmane blog
interface.

Link:
http://blog.gmane.org/gmane.org.user-groups.linux.ilugc

11. Search for answers for your questions/problems in
google.com or any other search engine before posting
your query to the mailing list.

If any of the above are not clear to you, Please read
the detailed list guidelines:

Link:
http://www.catb.org/~esr/faqs/smart-questions.html

12. Do not post messages in all capital letters. Mails
in CAPS is considered rude and is similar to shouting
during a conversation.

13. If you or your company is into GNU/Linux
migration, or you want help into GNU/Linux migration,
check here:
http://www.chennailug.org/wiki/GNU_Linux_migration

14. If you are looking for GNU/Linux distro CDs please
check here:
http://www.chennailug.org/wiki/CDs_for_Grab

15. Following these guidelines could be helpful
http://people.debian.org/~enrico/dcg/

16. Don't send season's greetings or birthday invites
to the group. Its not mailing list etiquette.

We must use the above guidelines to maintain better
readability, proper archives, efficient bandwidth
usage, etc.

--
Shakthi Kannan
http://www.shakthimaan.com

with the Yahoo! Search weather shortcut.

> What is the disk defragmentation utility of linux, as we used "Disk
> defragmentation" in windows?
>
>
Linux file systems do not need defragmentation. This site has a nice
explanation for that:
http://geekblog.oneandoneis2.org/index.php/2006/08/17/why_doesn_t_linux_need_defragmenting

But if you have mounted some FAT/NTFS partitions and want to defragment
them, I have no idea on how to do it.

From sbharathi at MidasComm.Com  Tue Apr  3 11:59:03 2007
From: sbharathi at MidasComm.Com (Bharathi Subramanian)
Date: Tue Apr  3 11:48:08 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] FYI: EMI Drops DRM, Apple to Sell Tracks Unencumbered
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0704031156400.3198-100000@bharathi.midascomm.com>

By now you may have heard today's surprising news: EMI and Apple
announced that the iTunes Music Store will offer EMI's entire catalog
for sale without DRM!  In a press release from Apple, Steve Jobs said,
???We think our customers are going to love this, and we expect to
offer more than half of the songs on iTunes in DRM-free versions by
the end of this year.??? (http://www.defectivebydesign.org/blog/969)

This is really big news.  We'll be sending Steve a the open letter
with over six thousand signatures and a thank you note with a
DefectiveByDesign.org T-Shirt in recognition of this step towards
eliminating DRM.  Thank you for signing the open letter and spreading
the word these last few weeks.

In the coming days and weeks we will see how the rest of the music
industry responds to EMI's move.  We'll keep you updated on ways you
can help eliminate DRM, not only in music, but also in operating
systems, hardware and libraries.

In Solidarity,
DRM Elimination Crew 
DefectiveByDesign.org is a project of the Free Software Foundation

Toufeeq Hussain wrote:
> On 4/2/07, Balavignesh <kbalavignesh@gmail.com> wrote:
>> Sound was working well but  Yester day while working the system
>> i have used hibernate. After reloading the system, sound not works for
>> me.
>> Everythings looks normal....

> 
> Make sure your soundcard is not muted. Does sound play in Windows ?
> Sometime people mute sound using the special Fn keys and it doesn't
> un-mute in Linux using the same key.

There's sometimes a lock file in /tmp/ or somewhere similar for alsa,
and that being stuck causes this issue. Restart (or stop, if necessary
killall, rmmod and modprobe the modules and then start) alsa and see if
that works?

Hibernate / suspend sometimes breaks sound, X settings etc in Acer
laptops (got a travelmate 290 myself so experienced this for myself)

	srs

From cyborg4k at yahoo.com  Wed Apr  4 06:59:57 2007
From: cyborg4k at yahoo.com (Shakthi Kannan)
Date: Wed Apr  4 07:00:17 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] boot loader
In-Reply-To: <69621d830704031105w1554b5d9h6a07836665bedfff@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <407613.78962.qm@web50207.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

Hi,

--- Zico <mailzico@gmail.com> wrote:
> And though i have
> installed OpenSuse
> later, so the loader of OpenSuse has taken it`s part
> for loading. 

The bootloader has installed itself to the MBR (first
512 bytes of hard disk). The bootloader loads after
BIOS POST.

> Now if i
> want to delete that OpenSuse, 

You can simply format the partition /dev/hda1.

> then how can i boot my
> Ubuntu again?
> I mean, do i need to install grub again?

Just add an entry to your Ubuntu in the bootloader
installed by OpenSuSE, if it was not added.

You might have to reinstall grub to the MBR. Google
for HOWTOs.

SK

--
Shakthi Kannan
http://www.shakthimaan.com

From prem at songbirdtech.com  Thu Apr  5 12:47:41 2007
From: prem at songbirdtech.com (Prem Kurian Philip)
Date: Thu Apr  5 12:48:46 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] GNU/Linux trouble
Message-ID: <1269.61.247.255.57.1175757461.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>


>> True, but try and tell that to the average person
>> when he finds his
>> printer is not working on Linux.

Vidhya Shankar L <lv.shankar@gmail.com> wrote:
>> Any user will expect ease of use from any software.
>> There is nothing wrong
>> if a person expects software to support his new
>> hardware.

Shakthi Kannan wrote:

>There is a false assumption made here.

> Why should he/she
> bother about the details while wanting  support for
> new hardware?

>Only when you clap both your hands, you can get sound.

>In other words, only when the hardware vendors release
>data sheets of their hardware, can software guys write
>drivers/software that can drive the hardware.

Talking about clapping with both hands - it is also the Linux OS
developer's responsibility to present a reasonably stable interface to the
driver developers which will allow the kernel developers to write drivers
which works across point releases of kernels for drivers in *both* binary
and source. That will make the job of writing drivers for Linux much
simpler.

Right now on Linux, every time the kernel changes even if it is a small
point release, the drivers will have to be recompiled. This does not make
any sense. On Windows, every time a service pack is applied or a hotfix is
applied, the users don't have to either recompile their entire set of
drivers or download binaries of the drivers for *that specific* kernel
point release.

If a driver is loaded on a computer with a 2.6 linux kernel, it should
work across all 2.6.x releases of the kernel without requiring a
recompile. But that is not the case right now.

Right now, Linux users have to wait for a long time after a latest
hardware comes out. They will have to hope that the company will either
release the complete hardware specs, and there is atleast one talented
kernel developer who has a personal interest in the hardware and is
willing to maintain the driver for a long time.

In my humble opinion, this is the wrong way of doing things. It should be
the hardware vendor's responsibility to write the drivers - they have the
greatest to gain from it. However, these companies may not be willing to
release the full specification of their device because they want to
protect their competitive edge for a while longer.

However since Linux OS developers have decided not to fix an interface for
the drivers, these companies will have to either a) release the driver as
source for the linux user to compile and load or b) release a binary
driver for each point release of the kernel. Nvidia has gotten around this
problem by creating their own kernel interface layer for the driver which
must be recompiled every time the kernel is changed. This allows their
binary-only drivers to be used on Linux machines without requiring
recompiles.

But that is strange - why would a company have to write its own kernel
interface layer? shouldn't this be part of the linux kernel? If it were a
part of the kernel, I am sure Linux hardware compatibility would be far
better than it is now.

Because Linux drivers are so closely tied up with the kernel, each version
(or point release) of the kernel source includes the source for many of
these drivers as well. So the binary of the linux kernel which ships as
default with many distributions of Linux will include a whole bunch of
drivers (including for some esoteric hardware) which the user does not
require at all.

Linux users are expected to recompile their kernel for their computers by
specifically turning off/on drivers which they don't want. These drivers
can be marked as compiled into the kernel or loadable as external modules
prior to recompilation.

Again, this would have been unnecessary if there was a clean interface
between the drivers and the rest of the kernel. It should be possible to
download the basic kernel (without the drivers) and then the drivers
separately or update the kernel without updating the drivers or update the
drivers (binaries) without changing the kernel.

That is not to mean that there won't be periodic changes to the driver
interface which will require companies to release drivers which work
against this new interface, but these changes shouldn't happen very often.

I am sure most users would be annoyed with Microsoft if every  update to
the kernel required the user to download and install a new set of drivers.
But for some reason we think it is ok if it worked that way on Linux.

Thoughts?

PK

Prem Kurian Philip wrote:
> Talking about clapping with both hands - it is also the Linux OS
> developer's responsibility to present a reasonably stable interface to the
> driver developers which will allow the kernel developers to write drivers
> which works across point releases of kernels for drivers in *both* binary
> and source. That will make the job of writing drivers for Linux much

Firstly, I think your email is based on many assumptions - a few that 
are not correct at all.

Then here is a question for you : When was the last time you did a 
kernel upgrade on Windows ?  afaik, XP had 1 kernel update, and that too 
was a patch into filesystem code to fix a security issue.

> Right now on Linux, every time the kernel changes even if it is a small
> point release, the drivers will have to be recompiled. 

This is absolutely not true. eg,. I've had a stable driver on my home 
server for the 3ware 9550sx working fine for almost 2 years now ( the 
machine has gone through, I've just checked, 11 kernels in the mean time 
). My laptop has been running ati prop drivers ( yea, i know :> ), since 
Nov 2005 and I've only had to rebuild them earlier this year, since I 
choose to rebase to a newer version of these drivers.

> If a driver is loaded on a computer with a 2.6 linux kernel, it should
> work across all 2.6.x releases of the kernel without requiring a
> recompile. But that is not the case right now.

well, thats a bit harsh - are you saying that no development work should 
happen on a kernel once its released ? Also, you seem to be confused 
about what constitutes the 2.x.x release factors.

> Right now, Linux users have to wait for a long time after a latest
> hardware comes out. 

why ? My Jmicron based kit was working on Linux before it worked on 
Windows...

OK, i know thats an exceptional case, but they do exist - but the time 
it takes from release -> usability is decreasing drastically. Specially 
a the userbase grows and more people become "visible" as users, hardware 
manufacturers will support more stuff ( classic case is the Xen people 
working with AMD when pacifia was evolving, and support was rolled in 
before the hardware even went into major production )

> In my humble opinion, this is the wrong way of doing things. It should be
> the hardware vendor's responsibility to write the drivers - they have the

politics and the blame games of 'your responsibility' and 'my 
responsibility' are usually shunned in the open source world. Hey, you 
think I am going to hang around waiting for someone to write a driver 
that I might want right now ? if I have the time, just do it myself and 
push it back upstream.

> greatest to gain from it. However, these companies may not be willing to
> release the full specification of their device because they want to
> protect their competitive edge for a while longer.

Thats again assumption and gross mass generalisations.

> But that is strange - why would a company have to write its own kernel
> interface layer? shouldn't this be part of the linux kernel? If it were a
> part of the kernel, I am sure Linux hardware compatibility would be far
> better than it is now.

there is dkms, which is open source, and if you really really do use a 
unstable distro and need that sort of functionality, feel free to use 
dkms, or one of the dozen odd kernel module management tools that are 
already out there.

you seem to be pointing fingers at these 'linux developers' but you seem 
to forget there is no single entity that is the linux-developers, 
contributions come in many and varied forms.

> Because Linux drivers are so closely tied up with the kernel, each version
> (or point release) of the kernel source includes the source for many of
> these drivers as well. So the binary of the linux kernel which ships as
> default with many distributions of Linux will include a whole bunch of
> drivers (including for some esoteric hardware) which the user does not
> require at all.

hey, you mean kind of like every distro also includes a lot of other 
packages that most people dont use ? You just distroyed the one thing 
that the debian guys are so proud of :)

> Linux users are expected to recompile their kernel for their computers by
> specifically turning off/on drivers which they don't want. These drivers
> can be marked as compiled into the kernel or loadable as external modules
> prior to recompilation.

thats a very wrong assumptions - most linux users are _not_ expected to 
recompile anything at all. Where did you get this misconception from ? 
eg. One place I do some work at, has about 500 machines running linux. 
Can you imagine everyone rebuilding their kernel and kernel modules 
eveyr so often ( and why? just for the sake of it ? ).

> I am sure most users would be annoyed with Microsoft if every  update to
> the kernel required the user to download and install a new set of drivers.
> But for some reason we think it is ok if it worked that way on Linux.
> 
> Thoughts?
> 

Linux isnt Windows, what you get with Linux comes with a few 'issues' 
but there are workarounds to most such issues.

Also, I dont see why people seem to moan and groan about driver support 
in Linux anymore. eg. OSX supports a very small subset of devices that 
Linux does - how many times do you see people on OSX complain about such 
things ? Finally, I remember a couple of years back sitting through this 
tech meet at a MS-Vendor, and what came across was that 90% of all 
crashes and reliability-of-platform issues were in the drivers and 
driver handling code. hey, dont know about you - but I like to not 
reboot my servers ( or even my workstations ) every day :)

- KB

Hi Prashanth,

Prashanth Mohan wrote:
>> think I am going to hang around waiting for someone to write a driver 
>> that I might want right now ? if I have the time, just do it myself 
>> and push it back upstream.
> 
> Simple users like myself often don't know the ABCs of writing Device 
> drivers. We expect them to be shipped on CDs or to work off the box.

you see therein lies the confusion - in an open source world, there are 
no real users and providers. Some of the recent commercial stuff does, 
however, feed this confusion much though :(

Everyone can contribute in some way or the other, and the circle / 
process works for everyone as long as there are enough people doing 
enough for the important bits, and there are some people still working 
on the fringe/related issues.

Having said that - I totally take on board the fact that there is much 
hardware out there that does not work well with linux at the moment - 
but the rate of adoption is changing ( increasing ) and hopefully this 
will get on top of stuff. Eg. its easy to see how the user density on 
server and enterprise linux users has already had a massive effect on 
hardware vendors who compete in these markets. And, on the Desktop sides 
as well there is major movement, but I suppose it takes longer there 
since h/w diversity is much greater there in a very competitive environment.

> OSX is meant to run on a mac and a mac _only_. The amount of hardware 
> that they need to support is limited to what they ship on their mac. 
> Apple would not provide support, if you were to install a modded version 
> of OSX on your PC.

Exactly, and therein is the issue : OSX users are only using a very 
small subset of h/w that is available out there ( and hey, its all 
mostly PC compatible now :D ) - so if you are looking at a solution, 
talk to a provider who certifies and provides hardware that works on 
Linux - its *one* option.

Having said that, I already know how hard that can be in the 
always-moving markets of suppliers in India :(

- KB

Karanbir Singh : http://www.karan.org/ : 2522219@icq

from all desktops" is unchecked.

From prem at songbirdtech.com  Fri Apr  6 13:09:19 2007
From: prem at songbirdtech.com (Prem Kurian Philip)
Date: Fri Apr  6 13:09:50 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Re: GNU/Linux trouble
Message-ID: <2535.122.164.65.175.1175845159.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>

*WARNING* - Long email

Karanbir Singh wrote:

>> Talking about clapping with both hands - it is also the Linux OS
>> developer's responsibility to present a reasonably stable interface tothe
>> driver developers which will allow the kernel developers to write drivers
>> which works across point releases of kernels for drivers in *both* binary
>> and source. That will make the job of writing drivers for Linux much
>> simpler.

>Firstly, I think your email is based on many assumptions - a few that
>are not correct at all.

Please state what those assumptions are.

>Then here is a question for you : When was the last time you did a
>kernel upgrade on Windows ?  afaik, XP had 1 kernel update, and that too
>was a patch into filesystem code to fix a security issue.

There have been multiple upgrades to the Windows XP kernel and the
subsystems. Service Pack 1, and Pack 2 were major upgrades, besides these
there have been many, many more.

Please see the following URL for a list.
http://support.microsoft.com/

The reason you didn't notice that these were kernel upgrades proves my
point that the MS update mechanism for kernels works well. If the kernel
upgrade had required you to recompile your drivers every single time, you
would have noticed it by now.


>> Right now on Linux, every time the kernel changes even if it is a small
>> point release, the drivers will have to be recompiled.

>This is absolutely not true. eg,. I've had a stable driver on my home
>server for the 3ware 9550sx working fine for almost 2 years now ( the
>machine has gone through, I've just checked, 11 kernels in the mean time
>). My laptop has been running ati prop drivers ( yea, i know :> ), since
>Nov 2005 and I've only had to rebuild them earlier this year, since I
>choose to rebase to a newer version of these drivers.

You can install compile drivers provided with *source* into the kernel if
you have the kernel headers without recompiling the entire kernel. But if
you don't have the source for the driver, you are out of luck.

Even Nvidia (and I think ATI too) ship binary drivers with multiple
precompiled kernel interface layer code - one for each point release of
the kernel. When a new kernel is released and if you don't have a
precompiled header for it, you must compile the kernel interface layer
code provided.

My point: If binary drivers can be supported by requiring companies to
provide their own kernel interface layers, won't it be simpler to just
provide the kernel interface layer as part of the kernel itself?

I am not a kernel developer. So if there are kernel developers here,
please enlighten if I have anything mixed up or if I am wrong.

>> If a driver is loaded on a computer with a 2.6 linux kernel, it should
>> work across all 2.6.x releases of the kernel without requiring a
>> recompile. But that is not the case right now.

>well, thats a bit harsh - are you saying that no development work should
>happen on a kernel once its released ? Also, you seem to be confused
>about what constitutes the 2.x.x release factors.

Did I say that or are you assuming that I did?

What I am saying is that the driver interface should be fixed, not the
kernel. It is possible to write a driver for Windows which will work
across service packs, kernel updates etc. Major changes to the kernel can
be made without changing the driver interface. On occassion when the
driver interface has to be changed, it should be. But it is unlikely that
each point release of the kernel requires such a change. The kernel
developers can try and keep the driver interface as stable as possible so
that even binary drivers can be used across kernel updates.

>> In my humble opinion, this is the wrong way of doing things. It should be
>> the hardware vendor's responsibility to write the drivers - they have the

>politics and the blame games of 'your responsibility' and 'my
>responsibility' are usually shunned in the open source world. Hey, you
>think I am going to hang around waiting for someone to write a driver
>that I might want right now ? if I have the time, just do it myself and
>push it back upstream.

Hardware manufacturers have an interest in selling their products and so
they will try and have drivers available for as many mainstream OSs as
possible. Is that a difficult to understand concept? The reason they can't
do that very effectively on Linux is because they have to provide the
source.

>> greatest to gain from it. However, these companies may not be willing to
>> release the full specification of their device because they want to
>> protect their competitive edge for a while longer.

>Thats again assumption and gross mass generalisations.

If I am making an assumption, specify it. The same applies for
generalizations.

Some questions for you:

a) Why don't you get the source drivers for ATI?
b) If that is not available, how is that you have not gotten around to
writing the drivers for your ATI card yet?
c) Oh, you mean ATI hasn't released the specs for it? Why not? (hint: see
my "assumption" above")

Your answers will explain my point.

>you seem to be pointing fingers at these 'linux developers' but you seem
>to forget there is no single entity that is the linux-developers,
>contributions come in many and varied forms.

Linux is not some amorphous entity which comes about (evolves) all by
itself without anyone making the decisions concerning its architecture.
Decisions about something like a fixed driver layer has been discussed
multiple times on the linux kernel developer lists, but for some reason it
has been decided not to stabilize on a driver interface because the linux
developers want the freedom to modify the interface anytime they want.

I can understand that they want this freedom to be able to make changes to
the interface, but they must contend with the fact that they are making
things much more inconvenient to users and to hardware vendors.

And yes, such as thing as a "user" does exist. Not everyone is going to
sit down and write a driver or make changes to the linux kernel. How often
have you done this yourself and this despite the fact that you perhaps
fall in the < 0.01  odd percentage of people out there perhaps with the
skills and the interest?

>Also, I dont see why people seem to moan and groan about driver support
>in Linux anymore. eg. OSX supports a very small subset of devices that
>Linux does - how many times do you see people on OSX complain about such
>things ?

This is an oft-quoted answer to a question about the driver interface so I
will address this.

1) It is a myth that Linux has more drivers than any other OS. What is
true is that Linux kernel *SHIPS* with more drivers than that comes with
any other OS. That is to say that the Windows installation CDs won't have
the same number of drivers as Linux.

But the point is that Windows does not *NEED* to ship with all these
drivers because the windows driver architecture allows hardware vendors to
ship binary drivers with their hardware on driver CDs.

2. OSX - OSX like windows also does not *NEED* to ship with all the
drivers. Also, OSX is shipped by Apple on the hardware they produce
themselves so they have greater control of what hardware to support.

Please note that I fully accept that I may be wrong about the benefit of
having a stable driver API. But if I am, I would like to hear the specific
reasons. Linux fanboy statements don't count as specific reasons.

Thanks for you patience,
PK

> Not to be nit-picky. But I believe, a copy into the directory is
> supposed to cause some instability. A wise old man advised me to symlink
> the .so file into the ~/.mozilla/plugins/ directory.

Once you do this, open a browser and enter "about:plugins" in the
address bar and see if the Flash Plugin is listed there. If "stuff"
still doesn't load, we have a problem.

-Toufeeq

blog @ http://www.toufeeq.net

From cyborg4k at yahoo.com  Sat Apr  7 09:58:23 2007
From: cyborg4k at yahoo.com (Shakthi Kannan)
Date: Sat Apr  7 09:58:46 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] want the help to run arm linux on qemu
In-Reply-To: <d489eaf10704062048k43e94a51u30bd4c698c789ca6@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <249595.33460.qm@web50203.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

Hi,

--- Ashok Kumar <ashok.grpmail@gmail.com> wrote:
> can any one summaries the steps to do this?

You didn't use any search engine? 

Debian on an emulated ARM machine:
http://www.aurel32.net/info/debian_arm_qemu.php

Running GNU/Linux for ARM processors under QEMU
http://nepotismia.com/linux/qemu/arm/

Google for "qemu for arm" for more examples.

To cross-compile your own kernel set the following in
the kernel Makefile:

ARCH=arm
CROSS_COMPILE=/path/to/arm-linux-gcc

In future, please use a search engine for answers
before posting a question.

SK

Shakthi Kannan
http://www.shakthimaan.com

Dear all

   Kenneth,I and Parthan conducted a two day workshop on FOSS to the
1 and 2 year student of MCA in VLB Janakiammal Engg College 
Coimbatore on 4 and 5th Apr 2007.

Kenneth inagurated the session with a talk on contributing to FOSS. 
We covered intro,installation basic gui-cli, visual programming,
networking, scripting langugage and ltsp. 

   The students were enthusiastic, the faculty and management very
supportive. I am sure to see growing use of FOSS in this college and
hope many of forthcoming final year projects will be in FOSS.

   On behalf of ILUGC I thank the management,Prof Thilagu HOD and
other faculty members for giving us an opportunity. I also thank
NRCFOSS for arranging this.

Subject: [Ilugc] flash player problem
In-Reply-To: <69621d830704051200g45c6f8fcreb2a6f7910519ed3@mail.gmail.com>
References: <69621d830704051200g45c6f8fcreb2a6f7910519ed3@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <e3dcb5360704070004sc7ddd8ag313aefb492cdf47f@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/6/07, Zico <mailzico@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> I have installed flash player for linux. But, i cannot load any flash
> "stuff" in firefox.


which OS are u using? normally when a plugin isn't available, firefox does
show them on the top of the browser instructing to install the missing
plugin. a button "Install Missing Plugin" should also be generally
displayed. clicking on it will search for the plugin (in this case its
flash) and it will be installed most of the cases. re-starting firefox
should make the plugin installed, functional.

If your OS is Ubuntu +, then from the terminal install the flash plugin in
Multiverse repository,

$ sudo apt-get install flashplugin-nonfree

this should help on re-starting the browser...

From srivasta at debian.org  Sun Apr  8 14:48:38 2007
From: srivasta at debian.org (Manoj Srivastava)

Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Forcing dpkg to ask for changed conffile questions in
	case of a reinstall
References: <9a9bfc170704071002o1caf5b77g19bf54ccb3648473@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <87zm5j6x75.fsf@glaurung.internal.golden-gryphon.com>

Hi,
On Sat, 7 Apr 2007 22:32:31 +0530, R Ramkumar said: 

> Hi!  When a package is upgraded and a configuration file changes as a
> result, dpkg usually asks the user whether the new conf file should be
> installed if it has local changes. Is it possible to make it ask this
> question even when dpkg is just reinstalling the same version? I know
> its possible to unpack the .deb file and replace/diff manually but
> would like to know if this is possible...

,----[  Manual page dpkg(1)  ]
|   confdef: If a conffile has been  modified  always  choose
|   the default action. If there is no default action it will
|   stop  to  ask  the   user   unless   --force-confnew   or
|   --force-confold is also been given, in which case it will
|   use that to decide the final action.
`----

        manoj

From pravi.a at gmail.com  Sun Apr  8 19:31:02 2007
From: pravi.a at gmail.com (Praveen A)
Date: Sun Apr  8 19:31:16 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Fwd: Etch in the hands of the Stable Release Managers
In-Reply-To: <20070408125412.GI25115@mails.so.argh.org>
References: <20070408125412.GI25115@mails.so.argh.org>

---------- Forwarded message ----------
From: Andreas Barth <aba@not.so.argh.org>
Date: ???? ?????? ? ??:??
Subject: Etch in the hands of the Stable Release Managers
To: debian-devel-announce@lists.debian.org


Hi,

You might read about now in debian-announce [1] that we have just
released. Please don't upload all your new software instantly, but
wait a few days until the archive is set up properly for Lenny.

At this point, we would like to thank all people who have helped to
release Debian 4.0 (Etch). Only through the work of the community we
were able to get to this point.

It's hard to write "thank you"s for a project as big as etch because there
are *so many* people involved in pulling this off, but they deserve
recognition for etch as much as we do -- people like the ftp team, the
installer team, the CD team, the mirror team, the porters, the release notes
editors, the translators, and an amazing group of "release minions" who
just worked wherever it was necessary like Steinar Gunderson, all of whose
contributions were essential for getting us to this point.

Special thanks to all those who have worked through the weekend for those
finishing touches that brought the release together, including Ryan, Anthony,
Frans, Steve McIntyre, Frank Lichtenheld, Mattias Wadenstein, Alexander Schmehl,
and many others.

And of course, thanks to the Debian Project as a whole, including
the innumerable mass of people who have provided patches, reported
bugs against testing or contributed in some other way.  This is your release,
friends.

We also want to apologize to the translations teams and thank them for
their work on the localisation of the release notes, our provided
deadlines were quite tough. Thanks for handling this so well.


Preparing the first point-release
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
There are already some packages known that will need an update in stable
soon.  Best known is the kernel, which has some annoying bugs with pending
fixes. If you are aware of any important bug that is waiting
in unstable and didn't make the release because of time constraints,
send a mail to debian-release@lists.debian.org.  The usual rules that
were in place for freeze exceptions are also mainly valid for the first point
release of the Etch life cycle. It will be possible to accept packages
directly from unstable to stable-proposed-updates. If you want the stable
release team to do that, wait with new uploads until you got a confirmation
that the package was accepted.

Also we might accept bug fixes to meet our release goal of LSB 3.1 compliance.


Continuing with Lenny
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
After the release is before the release. We don't have too specific plans
for the Lenny release cycle yet, but there are a couple of things that
are already known:

 * Lenny should be kept in a releaseable state most of the time (with the
   exception of breakages caused by one of the big transitions; but we
   should try to keep even these as small as possible).  This
   means that there are no excuses for not fixing an RC bug.  The problems
   that were uncovered by piuparts runs in the past two weeks have been
   mostly ignored for Etch, but they need to be fixed for Lenny.  These
   are trivial fixes, please apply them and upload soon.

 * Please coordinate your uploads a bit, so that the testing transition
   of packages stays possible.  Everybody uploading new software
   usually makes britney cry and hinders the transition of new versions.

 * We will work with release blockers and release goals again for the
   Lenny release cycle.  If you have anything that you feel belongs in
   one of these two categories, send a mail to -release, so that we can
   include it in our plans.  However, we want you to remember that
   -release is not a discussion list.

 * We want to restrict the release cycle for Lenny to less than 2 years,
   we want to discuss experiences of Etch first though to get a more
   accurate time planning.


We'll soon send a more in-detail information about what we think we learned
from Etch, and a few more things for Lenny. We'll also try to have an
Lenny-kick-off-meeting soon - we'll announce that in time as well, so that
anybody can feed us with ideas.


Now, we'll party and let the SRMs do the rest until Etch is out.  Thanks
again to all of you for the great support Debian got.


Yours Debian Release Team
Andi, Steve, Luk, Marc
--
http://release.debian.org/
[1] http://lists.debian.org/debian-announce/

Subject: [Ilugc] PCI-X to PCI-e convertion card
In-Reply-To: <709901.39053.qm@web54508.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <709901.39053.qm@web54508.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <a8f990120704082241m5a6d19abyc02a849e636f61f6@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/31/07, bala <egrpbala@yahoo.com> wrote:
>
>
>        One of our server running linux, only
> one PCI slot available that is PCI-X, we need
> PCI-e, is there any converter kind of thing
> available to convert from pci-x to pci-e,
> even though the performance is still PCI-X.


  It can't be done - PCI-e is a different bus standard
from PCI-X. In any event, it's trivial to swap out the
old motherboard and replace it with a new one
with PCI-e support, faster processor, more RAM, etc.

-Siva

Generally in our introductory talks, what we mainly cover is 
philosophical aspects of FOSS and try to make them understand the 
advantages. The talk "contribution to FOSS" was aimed at implying that 
what we call as FOSS is more about Contribution and Collaboration. It 
explained how things work and how things are done in what we call as FOSS.

The talk was small, simple and covered practical details of 
"Introduction to FOSS". I request Kenneth to make the presentation 
available so all those who did not attend your talk can get to know what 
you spoke about.

DEFAULT_UNICODE_FONT=latarcyrheb-sun16
# Also drdos8x16 is a good candidate.

case $# in
        2)
                /bin/setfont $1 -u $2
                ;;
        1)
                /bin/setfont $1
                ;;
        0)
                /bin/setfont $DEFAULT_UNICODE_FONT
                ;;
        *)
                echo "usage: unicode_start [font [unicode map]]"
                ;;
esac

this is what i have in my /bin/unicode_start file, i think my default
unicode font is not supporting

From sbharathi at MidasComm.Com  Tue Apr 10 09:32:09 2007
From: sbharathi at MidasComm.Com (Bharathi Subramanian)
Date: Tue Apr 10 09:21:10 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] [JOB] FSF Campaigns Manager
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0704100925530.6735-100000@bharathi.midascomm.com>

FSF Campaigns Manager

Job Description

The Campaigns Manager implements the FSF communications strategy and
works as part of a team to develop and implement issue campaigns and
community resources, acting as a spokesperson on matters of software
freedom. The Campaigns Manager handles writing, editing, speaking, and
research related to these activist and program efforts; coordinates
the GNU Chief Webmaster and the other webmaster volunteers to develop
FSF and GNU web sites; plans and implements proposals to increase
fundraising; and serves as a main point of contact between the
Foundation and the free software community.

Background

This is an opportunity to work for the organization that started the
GNU project, authored the GPL, and launched the free software
movement. Working as part of a team of two, you will help direct FSF
campaigns and communications strategy.

Requirements

* You must be a free software activist with a commitment to
  computer user freedom.

* Excellent written communication skills, able to demonstrate
  that you can work in a precise way whilst handling multiple
  tasks.

* A good public speaker with experience of handling press
  relations.

* Experience of organizing events and handling community
  relations in a diplomatic way.

* Possess the technical skills of a Webmaster.

* Candidates must support the principles of Software Freedom and
  demonstrate their knowledge of the policies and work of the
  Foundation.

Other Details

The job must be worked on-site at FSF's downtown Boston offices. An
on-site interview will be required with both the Executive Director
and FSF President.

This job is a union position. The salary is fixed at $50,057/year and
is non-negotiable. Other benefits include the following:

* Full family health coverage through Blue Cross/Blue Shield's HMO
  Blue program, and subsidized dental plan.
* Four weeks of paid vacation annually,
* Seventeen paid holidays annually,
* Public transit commuting cost reimbursement,
* 403(b) program through TIAA-CREF,
* Somewhat flexible work hours,

Resumes and cover letters must be submitted via email to
<hiring@fsf.org>. The email must contain the subject line, "Campaigns
Manager". Resumes should be attached in ASCII text or PDF. Cover
letters must be in ASCII directly in the body of the email message.
Resumes not sent via email or email submissions that do not follow
these instructions will be ignored.

Initial interviews will be held in the week commencing April 22.

--
John Sullivan
Campaigns Manager            | Phone: (617)542-5942 x23
51 Franklin Street, 5th Fl.  | Fax:   (617)542-2652	
Boston, MA 02110-1301 USA    | GPG:   AE8600B6

From rhariram at gmail.com  Tue Apr 10 13:12:13 2007
From: rhariram at gmail.com (R Hariram Aatreya)
Date: Tue Apr 10 13:12:26 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Tamil keyboard layout(s)?
In-Reply-To: <20070409224800.A27085@Snorkel.rtfm.net.au>
References: <20070409224800.A27085@Snorkel.rtfm.net.au>
Message-ID: <ad2a4f6d0704100042m58099a7aqb6f9a2a25e96c8fb@mail.gmail.com>

hi Doran,

On 4/9/07, Doran <aspect@snorkel.rtfm.com.au> wrote:
> A simple question for the list. Maybe.
  Well... it isn't :(

> What is the most well-known or appropriate Tamil keyboard layout to configure
> on a bilingual system? I have the "Tamil Unicode" keyboard working on Ubuntu,

  Most well-known : (among Tamil population) Remington layout (aka Old
Typewriter layout).
    This layout is the equivalent of qwert for English
    (inefficient & non-intutive but most widely used)

  Most appropriate : depends ...
       (for people familiar with English keyboard):
             Phonetic (on scim)
       (keyboards with the appropriate Tamil and English glyphs printed):
             Tamil99 (on scim?)

What you have as "Tamil Unicode" (uses xkb) maps
closer to what one might call as New Typewriter layout.

Now some *history* of Tamil Keyboard layouts:

* Remington layout (Old Typewriter layout)
  The initial Tamil typewriter layout was
  designed by Ramalingam Muttiah. (circa 1915)
  (see http://www.tamilnation.org/hundredtamils/muttiah.htm)
  This is has been the standard for typewriter.
  Most widely used layout.
  Typwriting institutes still use the mechanical typewriter
  (and hence this layout).

* New Typewriter layout:
  Changes were made to Remington layout to make it a bit intutive
  (probably during the advent of Electronic typewriters?).
  Except for a few geeks and my wife,
  I have'nt quite seen anyone else use this :)

* Inscript layout:
  Proposed by CDAC.
  Many systems using CDAC's *Gist* use this.
  I know of atleast one organization that use this extensively.

* Tamil99 layout:
  Proposed by Govt. of Tamil Nadu in 1999.
  For this layout, you can get "keyboards with the appropriate Tamil
  and English glyphs printed on their keycaps" at Chennai's Richie street.

* Phonetic layout:
  first created by Murasu. Meant for people familiar with English keyboard.


hari.

From rajanvn at gmail.com  Tue Apr 10 18:52:45 2007
From: rajanvn at gmail.com (Natarajan V)
Date: Tue Apr 10 18:52:58 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] [OT] Surprise, Microsoft Listed as Most Secure OS
Message-ID: <e977d4310704100622j3bbf3675if69198399043d53f@mail.gmail.com>

http://www.esecurityplanet.com/prevention/article.php/3667306

Microsoft is frequently dinged for having insecure products, with
security holes and vulnerabilities. But Symantec (Quote), no friend of
Microsoft, said in its latest research report that when it comes to
widely-used operating systems, Microsoft is doing better overall than
its leading commercial competitors.

The information was a part of Symantec's 11th Internet Security Threat
Report. The report, released this week, covered a huge range of
security and vulnerability issues over the last six months of 2006,
including operating systems.

The report found that Microsoft (Quote) Windows had the fewest number
of patches and the shortest average patch development time of the five
operating systems it monitored in the last six months of 2006.

During this period, 39 vulnerabilities, 12 of which were ranked high
priority or severe, were found in Microsoft Windows and the company
took an average of 21 days to fix them. It's an increase of the 22
vulnerabilities and 13-day turnaround time for the first half of 2006
but still bested the competition handily.

Red Hat Linux was the next-best performer, requiring an average of 58
days to address a total of 208 vulnerabilities. However, this was a
significant increase in both problems and fix time over the first half
of 2006, when there were 42 vulnerabilities in Red Hat and the average
turnaround was 13 days.

The one bright spot in all of this is that of the 208 Red Hat
vulnerabilities, the most of the top five operating systems, only two
were considered high severity, 130 were medium severity, and 76 were
considered low.

Then there's Mac OS X. Despite the latest TV ads ridiculing the
security in Vista with a Matrix-like Agent playing the UAC in Vista,
Apple (Quote) has nothing to brag about. Symantec found 43
vulnerabilities in Mac OS X and a 66 day turnaround on fixes.
Fortunately, only one was high priority.

Like the others, this is also an increase over the first half of the
year. For the first half of 2006, 21 vulnerabilities were found in Mac
OS X and Apple took on average 37 days to fix them.

Bringing up the rear were HP-UX from Hewlett Packard (Quote) and
Solaris from Sun (Quote). HP-UX had 98 vulnerabilities in the second
half of 06 and took 101 days to fix them. Sun, though, really dragged
its feet, taking on average 122 days to fix 63 vulnerabilities. It
wasn't doing much better in the first half of 06, either. It took 89
days to fix 16 vulnerabilities.

Alfred Huger, vice president of engineering for Symantec Security
Center, said the real problem is with Web applications, where
two-thirds of all vulnerabilities are found. Operating systems are
fairly minor, and despite the long time periods, the vendors are doing
"an ok job, just not stellar."

The response from vendor's mentioned in the report was mixed. A
Microsoft spokesperson issued a statement to internetnews.com that
said in part "As a part of this industry, Microsoft continues to adapt
to address these threats and continues to work with others in the
industry to protect customers as a whole."

Anuj Nayar, manager of Apple's Mac OS X and developer relations, would
only say "Apple takes security very seriously and has a great track
record of addressing vulnerabilities before they affect you."

Sun specifically disputed Symantec's data and conclusions in a
statement emailed to internetnews.com:

"Symantec's data on security vulnerabilities simply does not match
Sun's. We can't verify Symantec's sources and consider their report on
Sun inaccurate. From 7/1/06-12/31/06 we published 54 Security Sun
Alerts, of which 36 were for Solaris - substantially less the 63
Solaris vulnerabilities claimed in the Symantec report. Past analysis
of our vulnerability response shows we responded within five days for
the vast majority of vulnerabilities, but averages are skewed by a
small minority of 3rd party applications (or code) that are
included/bundled with Solaris. Sun responds to all reports of security
vulnerabilities, and we stand by our reputation and established track
record of responding to security vulnerabilities with Sun Alerts and a
quick turnaround time for patches.

Analyst Charles King with Pund-IT said Microsoft has had to be
aggressive about dealing with security issues because it's such a big
target. In that regard, the company has met the challenge.

"I think in a way that a culture of having been under attack for a
decade or more has led to the company taking a very proactive approach
to fixing those problems," he told internetnews.com. "In the last 24
months, they've taken a very aggressive stance toward the security of
their system. In review after review of Vista, despite its faults, the
security of the system has been considerably better than XP."

On Tuesday 10 Apr 2007 6:52:45 pm Natarajan V wrote:
> http://www.esecurityplanet.com/prevention/article.php/3667306

> Microsoft is frequently dinged for having insecure products, with
> security holes and vulnerabilities. But Symantec (Quote), no friend of
> Microsoft, said in its latest research report that when it comes to
> widely-used operating systems, Microsoft is doing better overall than
> its leading commercial competitors.

Two questins to be asked:
1) What were these vulnerabilities? How severe and how easy to activate?
2) Is the average a good indication of how much time it took?

If a high-priority issue took several weeks while a low-priority one took only 
days, or vice versa, how does this affect the study?

> During this period, 39 vulnerabilities, 12 of which were ranked high
> priority or severe, were found in Microsoft Windows and the company
> took an average of 21 days to fix them. It's an increase of the 22
> vulnerabilities and 13-day turnaround time for the first half of 2006
> but still bested the competition handily.

12 severe vulnerabilities vs only two for RedHat and one for OS X. That's 
something to measure also.

Without knowing more details of this study, I don't think it's prudent to come 
to any particular conclusions.

From mail-lists at karan.org  Wed Apr 11 07:32:29 2007
From: mail-lists at karan.org (Karanbir Singh)

Subject: [Ilugc] [JOB] Systems and Network Admin
Message-ID: <461C41B5.60609@karan.org>

Hi,

We ( DSource Plc )  are looking to hire 3 ( three ) sysadmins in India.
I am going to try and cover the details, briefly - feel free to send
questions my way; either onlist or offlist. Also, if you know someone
who might be interested,feel free to forward this email to them.

Job Spec:

While the job is primarily system admin and network management, there is
a fair bit of 'script coding' that is also required.

Required:

The Indian operation is just starting off, so initial work will require
a fair bit of remote 'pairing'. We are also going to be spending a few
months in India onsite at a client, so expect to be thrown right into
the firezone.

Technologies that should not be strange to you : Linux ( we are a 100%
CentOS / RHEL shop ), Xen, selinux, puppet ( and/or cfengine ), isp
grade networks ( routing, packet monitoring and performance testing ),
high performance database's, high density storage, large data volume
handling.

You *must* be able to write scripted code in ruby. Python and php are
also handy to know - but not required - since lots of our legacy stuff
is done in python and php ( dont ask, the php stuff was before my time
... ). We dont really care about what degree you have. we also dont care
if you got 60% or 90% in your exams or what certificates you carry along
with you. But, you need to be able to communicate in English, must have
atleast a few years of working in a related environment. Common sense
and an ability to rationally think are, however, critical! General
sysadmin skills are essential ( we will test you on these ). People with
a well thumbed copy of 'unix power tools' should definitely apply!

All assignments are permanent, full time and come with the govt
requirements for benefits etc.

Location:

We are looking for people based in Pune, Delhi and Chennai as a base.
However, for people based in Delhi and Chennai there will be a frequent
( not exceeding 4 working days / month ) requirement to travel to Pune.

Type of Employer:

The Company is a infrastructure management consulting service, where we
offer systems and network services to clients. We have 27 people working
full time and 8 people part-time in the UK. And a further 6 people in
Australia. Agile and XP practises are a cornerstone of the management
style ( in that there is really no management, the entire organisation
tree is very flat ).

Salary and Benefits:

You get paid pretax's Rs.80,000/- a month. We expect you to work 38 hrs
per week. We also only work half-days on Wednesdays. There maybe a
requirement to work weekends, but only after suitable advance notice (
mostly to handle infrastructure outage that cant happen during the work
week ). There is a 6 months trial run period, wherein you get to see
what we do and if you want to work with us, and we get to see if we want
you working with us.

Interview process:

1. Send me a CV.
2. We call you for a quick chat on the phone. ( 20 min ).
3. Have a longer  ( 3 hrs estimate ) sysadmin 'pair' session.
4. Fix a time and have a longer ( 1 hr ) developer 'pair' session.
5. People get hired. ( 1st week of June 2007 )

Regards,

- KB

[1] - Agencies and head hunters are not welcome to reply to me, we dont
and wont hire people through agencies.

From cyborg4k at yahoo.com  Wed Apr 11 07:47:09 2007
From: cyborg4k at yahoo.com (Shakthi Kannan)
Date: Wed Apr 11 07:47:29 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] build filesystem
Message-ID: <263671.64764.qm@web50201.mail.re2.yahoo.com>

Hi,

--- Zico <mailzico@gmail.com> wrote:
> If i want to build a tiny filesystem.... then what
should i do?

Configure and build busybox to create the necessary
executables and shell that you want. For example, it
will create bin/, sbin/, /usr/bin, /usr/sbin
directories.

You can then add the other directories like /var, /etc
as per your need.

This is the method to build a customized filesystem
image for use in embedded systems.

SK

--
Shakthi Kannan
http://www.shakthimaan.com

From sbharathi at MidasComm.Com  Wed Apr 11 18:18:53 2007
From: sbharathi at MidasComm.Com (Bharathi Subramanian)
Date: Wed Apr 11 18:07:31 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Fwd: LJ Tech Tip
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0704111811470.13112-100000@bharathi.midascomm.com>

LJ TECH TIP:

This tech tip comes from the book Beginning Ubuntu Linux by Keir
Thomas (Apress):

After using Ubuntu for some time, you might find that the disk begins
to get full.  You can keep an eye on disk usage by using the following
command in a GNOME Terminal window:

   df -h

This will show the free space in terms of megabytes or gigabytes, and
also expressed as a percentage figure. If the disk does start to get
full, you can take some steps to make space more available.

* Emptying the /tmp Folder

An easy way to regain disk space is to empty the /tmp folder.  As with
the Windows operating system, this is the folder in which temporary
data is stored.  Some applications clean up after themselves, but
others don't, leaving behind many megabytes of detritus.

Because the /tmp folder is accessed practically every second the
system is up and running, to empty it safely, it's necessary to switch
to run level 1.  This insures few other programs are running and
avoids the risk of deleting data that's in use.  The following series
of commands will switch to run level 1, empty the /tmp folder, and
then reboot afterwards:

      sudo killall gdm
      [login with your username and password]
      sudo init 1
      rm -rf /tmp/*
      reboot

Tip: On a similar theme, don't forget to empty the desktop
Wastebasket. This can hold many megabytes of old data.

* Emptying the Cache of Package Files

You might also choose to clear out the Advanced Packaging Tool (APT)
cache of old .deb package files.  On a system that has been very
frequently updated, this can free many megabytes (possibly gigabytes)
of space.

You can empty the cache by typing the following command in a GNOME
Terminal window:

     sudo rm -f /var/cache/apt/archives/*.deb

Caution! Be careful to type the command to empty the APT cache exactly
as it is written.  Even inserting an additional space can lead to very
bad consequences.

If you want to restore any packages later on, simply locate them in
the Synaptic Package Manager list, click the check box, and click Mark
for Reinstallation.  This will cause the package to be downloaded,
installed and configured.

HTH :)
--
Bharathi S

I think he is asking about the EXIF metadata.
If you use flickr, you can view an image's EXIF data by seeing the

All the details like - camera, exposure, aperture, focal length, etc
are all stored in the jpeg.
Is there any tool to change that data?

Yes. But for requesting CDs, you may not require to upload your
SSH/PGP keys. The keys are required when you are going to share your

integrated with launchpad. The keys are also required when you want to
become the member.

for requesting free CDs from feisty+1 release!

From akumar at ee.iitm.ac.in  Thu Apr 12 09:16:36 2007
From: akumar at ee.iitm.ac.in (Kumar Appaiah)
Date: Thu Apr 12 09:07:22 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] IIT Madras Linux Users' group
Message-ID: <20070412034636.GA9772@localhost>

Dear ILUGC,

For the past 6-7 months, we have had an unofficial IIT Madras Linux
Users' group. The group is, basically, for residents and students of
IIT Madras who want to get a head start with using GNU/Linux and other
free/open source software in IIT Madras. The group has been lively,
and it is heartening to see even first year students
participating. Though there's nothing official about it, we do try to
meet up and talk about some things. For details, see:

http://groups.google.co.in/group/linuxusers_iitm/about

Regarding the success of this attempt, I strongly believe that it is
because students are allowed to access the internet from the
hostels. That, I feel, has allowed Googling and encouraged students to
try things out on their own machine, rather than depend on a machine
installed at a computer centre, where you can't (su)do whatever you
want.

And, of course, many thanks to Prof. Sriram, who's supported this
attempt, and has been maintaining the mirror at ftp.iitm.ac.in, which
is indispensable for Debian and Ubuntu users at IITM. Also, thanks to
Bharathi for helping out in the group, and ILUGC in general for
teaching some of us some things on and off the list.

Kumar
-- 
Kumar Appaiah,
462, Jamuna Hostel,
Indian Institute of Technology Madras,
Chennai - 600 036

On Wed, Apr 11, 2007 at 10:08:25PM +0530, Srinivasan R wrote:
> I think he is asking about the EXIF metadata.
 [...]
> Is there any tool to change that data?

This is why I love apt:

$ apt-cache search exif
exif - command-line utility to show EXIF information in JPEG files
exiftags - Utility to read Exif tags from a digital camera JPEG file
exiftran - transform digital camera jpeg images
jhead - manipulate the non-image part of Exif compliant JPEG files
libjpeg-progs - Programs for manipulating JPEG files
 (among many others)

jhead looks the most promising. "apt-cache show jhead" provides:
 Upstream is: http://www.sentex.net/~mwandel/jhead/

YMMV, etc.

On Thu, 2007-04-12 at 12:33 +0530, Harish S wrote:
> 1) 15" XGA TFT LCD with Wireless LAN
> 2) 15" SXGA+ TFT                                 -- A $50 extra for this one
> 

I have a R60 with 15" XGA and I don't see any shortcomings with the
display.
AFAIK, SXGA offers a higher resolution at 1280 x 1024.

The chipset I have is Intel 945GM and X.org has drivers for it, so I had
no problems.
Check if X.org has drivers for the chipset that you are choosing.

> 1) ThinkPad 11a/b/g Wi-Fi wireless LAN Mini-PCIe US/EMEA/LA/ANZ
> 2) Intel PRO/Wireless 3945ABG (US/CAN/LA/AP) --- $5 extra for this one..
> 

The default Thinkpad WLAN chipset is an Atheros chipset.
Mine has it and I have absolutely no problems using it.
I have used it in 11b and 11g modes, with WPA2.
Ubuntu has introduced the restricted drivers policy, so installing
madwifi drivers for Atheros' chipsets is a breeze now.

> and  also want to know what would be sufficient/recommended to run
> compiz...among the below
> 1) Intel Graphics Media Accelerator 950, 1394
> 2) ATI Mobility Radeon X1300 64MB,1394
> 3) ATI Mobility Radeon X1400 128MB, 1394
> 

I haven't used Compiz or Beryl extensively, though I did try it once
and it ran well using the onboard Intel 945GM chipset.
Choosing ATI would surely enhance the experience, I guess.
And maybe, ATI's tradition of horrible linux drivers would change after
AMD's acquisition.

Sujith.

Some of the R-series Thinkpads do come without windows pre-installed (I
got my R51 this way) - just ask them if they could provide the system
without Windows - normally they should have that option. In this case,
it will only come with DOS.

Thinkpads work very well with Linux - haven't heard anyone who I know
who got a Thinkpad complain about Linux compatibility - just get the
laptop without Windows, load up Ubuntu and you're good to go. Things
like hibernate, suspend, etc should just work.

On 4/12/07, Vamsee Kanakala <vamlists@gmail.com> wrote:

> Thinkpads work very well with Linux - haven't heard anyone who I know
> who got a Thinkpad complain about Linux compatibility - just get the
> laptop without Windows, load up Ubuntu and you're good to go. Things
> like hibernate, suspend, etc should just work.

The R51/R52 are both IBM Models. The first R series model that's
designed by Lenovo is the R60. There are significant changes,
including some blatantly obvious and poor (IMHO) elements such as USB
ports on the right side, very plasticky and cheap-feeling build of the
keyboard and some buttons (like the sound buttons) makes

My point is that the R60 is not just a newer version of the R52 - it
is a redesigned machine that Lenovo wants to use to target the same
market segment as the R52 (folks on a budget). As such you should be
careful not to generalize linux-compatibility issues across the entire
R Series.

-- 
Sriram Karra

From f2005180 at bits-pilani.ac.in  Thu Apr 12 15:16:17 2007
From: f2005180 at bits-pilani.ac.in (Karthik Ramgopal)
Date: Thu Apr 12 15:14:28 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: ilugc Digest, Vol 35, Issue 31
In-Reply-To: <20070412084159.9C8991DB381@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
References: <20070412084159.9C8991DB381@www.ae.iitm.ac.in>
Message-ID: <1176371177.16585.4.camel@localhost.localdomain>

Dear Harish,
             ATI Proprietary Linux drivers are great and offer very
smooth performance with high frame rates. The only problem is that they
lack the support for Composite Extension, needed for AIGLX (Indirect
rendering). Even the Open Source radeon driver doesnt support the R500
series (to which both X1300 and X1400 belong). I use a X1800XT with XGL
and ATI Propreitary fglrx drivers here. Both Beryl and Compiz run
smoothly. GMA 950 has good open source driver support with the i810
driver and also supports AIGLX. But, the chipset is a damn slow one, and
few effects may be slow like Rotating cube with transperancy enabled,
water effects, snow effects and all. Especially water and snow as they
use pixel shaders, which are a sore point for the GMA 950. I'd say go
for the X1400. ATI Rocks.. :)

Regards,

Karthik

From mail-lists at karan.org  Fri Apr 13 16:26:38 2007
From: mail-lists at karan.org (Karanbir Singh)
Date: Fri Apr 13 17:29:01 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] Re: Re: GNU/Linux trouble
In-Reply-To: <2535.122.164.65.175.1175845159.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>
References: <2535.122.164.65.175.1175845159.squirrel@www.songbirdtech.com>
Message-ID: <461F61E6.6050509@karan.org>

Hi,

Prem Kurian Philip wrote:
> Please state what those assumptions are.
I thought they were clear in my last email, anyway ..

> There have been multiple upgrades to the Windows XP kernel and the
> subsystems. Service Pack 1, and Pack 2 were major upgrades, besides these
> there have been many, many more.

Actually, you will find that very very few patches are directly made to 
the kernel itself - the Windows kernel runs very different from the 
Linux one. Also, technically SP1 and SP2 were considered to be different 
releases as far as I know. And I do recall there being a lot of noise 
about drivers not working on SP2 when it came out initially. Sort of 
like the new Vista stuff - its taken them almost a year and half to get 
*some* of the drivers working right, a lot of hardware out there is 
still not really running ok on Vista.

> The reason you didn't notice that these were kernel upgrades proves my
> point that the MS update mechanism for kernels works well.

You have this very wrong. MS dont update and add functionality at that 
level of the system once its released. You never see the -devel or 
-trunk version of the Windows system level at all. you only see whats 
been released and called stable from them. What you are doing here is 
comparing an evolutionary process of the Linux kernel development to the 
waterfall process of the Windows system.

>> This is absolutely not true. eg,. I've had a stable driver on my home
>> server for the 3ware 9550sx working fine for almost 2 years now ( the
> 
> You can install compile drivers provided with *source* into the kernel if
> you have the kernel headers without recompiling the entire kernel. But if
> you don't have the source for the driver, you are out of luck.

It seems from your statements that you havent really ever used a stable 
distro before ? Could I ask what distro you really use ?

I actually meant what I said, I have a single binary kernel module that 
drives my 3ware 9550, without needing a recompile, for 2 years and its 
seem quite a few kernels. I dont even need to do anything at all beyond 
a yum update kernel. The machine has no kernel-devel or kernel sources ( 
also, you dont usually need the complete kernel-sources to build 
drivers, you should only need the headers - there are a few exceptions 
though )

> When a new kernel is released and if you don't have a
> precompiled header for it, you must compile the kernel interface layer
> code provided.

Ok, so you are completely wrong and mis-informed about this. ( as I type 
this from my Acer Ferrari 4005, running an ATI prop driver, that I 
_didnt_ need to recompile or do anything to, when I upgraded my laptop 
last night from centos-5/beta to centos-5/final x86_64 ).

Also, whats a pre-comiled header ?

> My point: If binary drivers can be supported by requiring companies to
> provide their own kernel interface layers, won't it be simpler to just
> provide the kernel interface layer as part of the kernel itself?

Your point is mostly irrelevant since its based on an assumption that is 
not really correct at all, might I even dare call it FUD spreading ?

> I am not a kernel developer. So if there are kernel developers here,
> please enlighten if I have anything mixed up or if I am wrong.

You mean like the linux kabi :)

>> well, thats a bit harsh - are you saying that no development work should
>> happen on a kernel once its released ? Also, you seem to be confused
>> about what constitutes the 2.x.x release factors.
> 
> Did I say that or are you assuming that I did?

Not in exact terms, but you implied it.

> What I am saying is that the driver interface should be fixed, not the
> kernel. It is possible to write a driver for Windows which will work
> across service packs, kernel updates etc. 

Ok, again you seem to be comparing oranges and apples. eg, a driver 
written for win98 is not really going to work on win vista. ( iirc, it 
was driving abstraction that held up vista work for a fair portion of 
its 5 year delay to release ) - so expecting a 2.4 driver to work with 
2.6 is ... maybe a bit too extreme.

While it would be nice to happen, and yes - there are things that might 
be done to make it happen, feel free to submit patches.

> Hardware manufacturers have an interest in selling their products and so
> they will try and have drivers available for as many mainstream OSs as
> possible. Is that a difficult to understand concept? The reason they can't
> do that very effectively on Linux is because they have to provide the
> source.

Not they dont, have you seen the souces for nvidia or ati ?  And while 
these companies might get some stick from the linux users, one thing 
that people dont say is that these drivers dont work ( well, I dont 
anyway and I have both nvidia and ati installed all over the place ).

You are also grossly generalizing when it comes to hardware vendors. Not 
  everyone even wants to support Linux or any non windows product base 
since they lack the technical ability to do so, and the users are not 
asking for the support - remember this is not windows land where one 
might expect drivers from hardware vendors, we prefer a driver in the 
included kernel. hell, most people will frown upon vendors who are not 
pushing the driver upstream for adoption - and guess what ? once that 
does get adopted - no more worries about hardware not working, whatever 
stable / unstable / head / branch you might be using!

> If I am making an assumption, specify it. The same applies for
> generalizations.

hopefully I would have cleared out some of these in the email now ?

> Some questions for you:
> 
> a) Why don't you get the source drivers for ATI?

to be honest with you - as long as they dont push for upstream adoption 
( and going by the conversations we had with ATI a few weeks back, some 
serious wackage will need to happen before they even think about it ) - 
I dont really care. Vendors on Linux have 2 options, the one most 
preferred by users and linux (ab)users is of upstream adoption. However 
if companies choose not to do that - guess what ? its a free world.... 
they can choose to support their hardware from the outside. As long as 
things work, I am fine with that.

> b) If that is not available, how is that you have not gotten around to
> writing the drivers for your ATI card yet?

why would I even want to ? everything just works for me anyway... yes, I 
am using the ati prop drivers on my laptop. I have suspend to ram, 
hibernate to drive, I can dual head my laptop with no problems ( hey 
even tripple head it! ), the wifi card works out of the box ( bcm43xx ), 
bluetooth works, tv-out works... i even get 11,000+ on glxgears ( 
important, since I do fancy a bit of Wolf3D:ET at times )

> c) Oh, you mean ATI hasn't released the specs for it? Why not? (hint: see
> my "assumption" above")

humm...

> Your answers will explain my point.

ok, so we've established that your point is mostly irrelevant.

> has been decided not to stabilize on a driver interface because the linux
> developers want the freedom to modify the interface anytime they want.

so stop using upstream kernels. And if it really bothers you so much, 
fork it. Like i said before, its a free world.

> I can understand that they want this freedom to be able to make changes to
> the interface, but they must contend with the fact that they are making
> things much more inconvenient to users and to hardware vendors.

You seem confused about exactly what constitutes an 'upstream' 
development process and what is the user deliverable 'distro'. While in 
a lot of ( Majority ? ) cases they might seem fused, in most of the 
linux user world people dont use upstream. ( gentoo [ab]users excluded 
*duck* )

> And yes, such as thing as a "user" does exist. Not everyone is going to
> sit down and write a driver or make changes to the linux kernel. How often
> have you done this yourself and this despite the fact that you perhaps
> fall in the < 0.01  odd percentage of people out there perhaps with the
> skills and the interest?

Havent made double figures since I left the job that required me to, but 
I did work with the team that made it to 9 drivers. Also, I've done a 
lot of stabalisation work with the ahci stuff ( specifically jmicron and 
areca hardware ).

> 1) It is a myth that Linux has more drivers than any other OS. What is
> true is that Linux kernel *SHIPS* with more drivers than that comes with
> any other OS. That is to say that the Windows installation CDs won't have
> the same number of drivers as Linux.

yes, the user expectation is very different on Windows and Linux. Most 
linux distro deliver a complete user experience in a consolidated manner.

> But the point is that Windows does not *NEED* to ship with all these
> drivers because the windows driver architecture allows hardware vendors to
> ship binary drivers with their hardware on driver CDs.

again, you are very wrong on this - Windows does ship with a fair few 
drivers, and I'll try to explain why this is. When WinXP went completely 
bottoms up and prompted MS to look at SP1, a team of analysts were 
brought in to find out exactly what was going on and why the system was 
so unstable. The findings of these people were a straight pointer to 
badly done drivers from third party vendors. Most hardware vendors dont 
actually write their own driers, they farm the jobs out to consultants 
and outsourcing companies. And *this* was the biggest problem with 
stability that MS had to contend with for a long time.

on the other hand, apart from one exceptionally wierd SMC sata hotswap 
bay driver that seems to leek memory and needs a reload every 4 to 5 
months, I've not had any issues with linux drivers. And before you say 
it, I know its a generalisation - but, I *do* use a lot of kit, some in 
obscure setups too.

> 2. OSX - OSX like windows also does not *NEED* to ship with all the
> drivers. Also, OSX is shipped by Apple on the hardware they produce
> themselves so they have greater control of what hardware to support.

that is exactly what my point was, they ship commodity hardware mostly, 
things that work fine on PC's a well - but the user base actually looks 
up what works before going out to buy it!

> Please note that I fully accept that I may be wrong about the benefit of
> having a stable driver API. But if I am, I would like to hear the specific
> reasons. Linux fanboy statements don't count as specific reasons.

I'd suggest the best way to find out is to actually use this thing, I am 
starting to wonder if you even use linux at all, and that too in a 
stable environment where these things matter.

- KB

ps: I know that some of the stuff is repeated in my email, just thought 
it was needed at the place to highlight the issue.

From anandsaha at gmail.com  Sat Apr 14 22:11:06 2007
From: anandsaha at gmail.com (Anand Saha)
Date: Sat Apr 14 22:11:14 2007
Subject: [Ilugc] [TIP] image viewing on command line
Message-ID: <154eca020704140941u52ca1e59wad79e2f2441d4807@mail.gmail.com>

I came across this gem of a tool to view images when you are in
command line mode. and if you are a cli person, this is a blessing.

the tool is fbi. 'apt-get install fbi' will install it on a debian based
system.

to use fbi, you need to pass the 'vga=' param to kernel to specify
resolution and depth for framebuffer. i use 'vga=0x315' for 800x600
res. and 24 bit depth. (edit your lilo.conf or menu.lst)

now once you reboot (if you added the vga option), you can invoke
fbi from command line with the image name as an argument, to
view the image. however it can do more than that. man fbi.

my original itch was to be able to view images while using lynx. now, once
fbi installed, and when you are in lynx interface, go to options and do
the following two changes:
1. Set X-Display to 'fbi' (type in fbi)
2. Set 'Show image' to 'as links'

Save it. (unfortunately the above two options do not get written to lynx
conf file. you need to set it for evey session with lynx)

Now lynx will start showing the images as links. navigate to the image
link and press right arrow. you will be able to see the image - all in
command line.

cheers,
saha
--

Hi,

--- Ashok Kumar <ashok.grpmail@gmail.com> wrote:
> is all the usb devices connected with system shares
> major numbers?
> is it differentiated by only minor numbers?

Yes.
 
> how it was numbered in 2.4series kernel?

devices.txt:
http://lxr.linux.no/source/Documentation/devices.txt?v=2.4.20

> how it is numberd in 2.6 series kernel?

Uses sysfs. device.txt:
http://lxr.linux.no/source/Documentation/devices.txt

USB LDD3:
http://lwn.net/images/pdf/LDD3/ch13.pdf

SK
